一	<b>one</b>	In Chinese characters, the number&nbsp;<b>one</b>&nbsp;is laid on its side, unlike the Roman numeral I which stands upright. As you would expect, it is written from left to right.	1	1	
二	<b>two</b>	Like the Roman numeral II, which reduplicates the numeral I, the kanji for&nbsp;<b>two</b>&nbsp;is a simple reduplication of the horizontal stroke that means one. The order of writing goes from above to below, with the first stroke slightly shorter.	2	2	
三	<b>three</b>	"And like the Roman numeral III, which triples the numeral I, the kanji for&nbsp;<b>three</b>&nbsp;simply triples the single horizontal stroke. In writing it, think of ""1 + 2 = 3"" (一 + 二&nbsp;= 三) in order to keep the middle stroke shorter."	3	3	
四	<b>four</b>	<div><b>Four&nbsp;</b>year-olds often put their foot/<i>human legs</i>&nbsp;(儿) in their&nbsp;<i>mouth</i>&nbsp;(口), but they are too young to realize.</div>	5	4	
五	<b>five</b>	三 + two vertical lines = 5.	4	5	
六	<b>six</b>	Indicating 6 parts of the human body: head, body, 2 arms, 2 legs.	4	6	
七	<b>seven</b>	This kanji is our good old<strong>&nbsp;seven</strong>, turned upside down.	2	7	
八	<b>eight</b>	"""はち"" (eight) begins with the sound of the&nbsp;katakana ""ハ"", and it's approximately the same character."	2	8	
九	<b>nine</b>	Kinda of looks like the kanji of 1 一 with the kanji of 8 八 added. So think of it as&nbsp;一＋八＝九	2	9	
十	<b>ten</b>	Turn this character 45 degrees either way and you have the x used for the Roman numeral&nbsp;<b>ten</b>.	2	10	
口	<b>mouth</b>	Like several of the first characters we shall learn, the kanji for&nbsp;<b>mouth</b>&nbsp;is a clear pictograph. Since there are no circular shapes in the kanji, the square must be used to depict the circle.	3	11	
日	<b>day</b>	Open the window, new<strong>&nbsp;day</strong>.	4	12	
月	<b>month</b>	A<strong>&nbsp;month</strong>&nbsp;is longer than a <i>day</i> (日).	4	13	
田	<b>rice field</b>	<em>Ten</em> (十)<strong>&nbsp;rice field</strong>s can feed many&nbsp;<em>mouths&nbsp;</em>(口).	5	14

今	<b>now</b>	<div>The final stroke of this kanji is a rare shape, which we have not met before and will only meet in this character and others that include it as a primitive. We are more accustomed to seeing it straightened out as part of other shapes - for instance, as the second stroke of&nbsp;<i>mouth</i>&nbsp;(口). If you need any help at all with this character, you may picture it as two hands of a clock pointing to what time it is&nbsp;<b>now</b>. By&nbsp;<i>comparing</i>&nbsp;(比) their drawings to real&nbsp;<b>deer</b>, Stone Age people hoped to acquire power over the animal in the hunt; and by&nbsp;<i>comparing</i>&nbsp;(比) themselves to the&nbsp;<b>deer</b>, to take on that animal's characteristics.
